(controlling owner/<a href="/wiki/Chairman" title="Chairman">Chairman</a>/<a href="/wiki/CEO" class="mw-redirect" title="CEO">CEO</a>)<br /><a href="/wiki/Matt_Dolan" title="Matt Dolan">Matt Dolan</a> href="/wiki/Mike_Chernoff_(baseball)" title="Mike Chernoff (baseball)">Mike Chernoff</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">President of Baseball Operations</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Chris_Antonetti" title="Chris Antonetti">Chris Antonetti</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Cleveland Indians</b> are an American professional <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> team based in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland, Ohio</a>. The Indians compete in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) as a member club of the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL) <a href="/wiki/American_League_Central" title="American League Central">Central</a> division. Since <a href="/wiki/1994_in_baseball" title="1994 in baseball">1994</a>, they have played at <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Field" title="Progressive Field">Progressive Field</a>. The team\'s <a href="/wiki/Spring_training" title="Spring training">spring training</a> facility is at <a href="/wiki/Goodyear_Ballpark" title="Goodyear Ballpark">Goodyear Ballpark</a> in <a href="/wiki/Goodyear,_Arizona" title="Goodyear, Arizona">Goodyear, Arizona</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Since their establishment as a major league franchise in 1901, the Indians have won two <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> championships: in <a href="/wiki/1920_World_Series" title="1920 World Series">1920</a> and <a href="/wiki/1948_World_Series" title="1948 World Series">1948</a>, along with 10 Central Division titles and six <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_League_pennant_winners" title="List of American League pennant winners">American League pennants</a>. The Indians\' current World Series championship drought is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_franchise_postseason_droughts#Longest_current_World_Series_championship_drought" title="List of Major League Baseball franchise postseason droughts">longest active drought</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The name "Indians" originated from a request by club owner <a href="/wiki/Charles_Somers" title="Charles Somers">Charles Somers</a> to baseball writers to choose a new name to replace "Cleveland Naps" following the departure of <a href="/wiki/Nap_Lajoie" title="Nap Lajoie">Nap Lajoie</a> after the 1914 season. The name referenced the nickname "Indians" that was applied to the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Spiders" title="Cleveland Spiders">Cleveland Spiders</a> baseball club during the time when <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sockalexis" title="Louis Sockalexis">Louis Sockalexis</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a>, played in Cleveland.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  Common <a href="/wiki/List_of_baseball_nicknames" title="List of baseball nicknames">nicknames</a> for the Indians include the "Tribe" and the "Wahoos", the latter being a reference to their logo, <a href="/wiki/Chief_Wahoo" title="Chief Wahoo">Chief Wahoo</a>. Also, the team\'s mascot is named "Slider."\n</p><p>The franchise originated in <a href="/wiki/Grand_Rapids,_Michigan" title="Grand Rapids, Michigan">Grand Rapids, Michigan</a>, in 1894 as the Grand Rapids Rustlers, a minor league team that competed in the <a href="/wiki/Western_League_(1885%E2%80%931899)" title="Western League (1885\xe2\x80\x931899)">Western League</a>. The team then relocated to Cleveland in 1900 and changed its name to the Cleveland Lake Shores. The Western League itself changed its name to the American League while continuing its minor league status. One of the American League\'s eight charter franchises, the major league incarnation of the club was founded in Cleveland in <a href="/wiki/1901_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1901 Major League Baseball season">1901</a>. Originally called the Cleveland Bluebirds, the team played in <a href="/wiki/League_Park" title="League Park">League Park</a> until moving permanently to <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Stadium" title="Cleveland Stadium">Cleveland Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/1946_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1946 Major League Baseball season">1946</a>. At the end of the 2018 season, they had a regular season franchise record of <abbr title="9,384 wins, 8,968 losses">9,384\xe2\x80\x938,968</abbr> (.511). On September 14, 2017, the Cleveland Indians won their 22nd straight game, which extended the new American League record they set the previous game, while also becoming the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_longest_winning_streaks" title="List of Major League Baseball longest winning streaks">longest winning streak</a> with no ties in Major League Baseball history.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#1994\xe2\x80\x932001:_New_beginnings"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">1994\xe2\x80\x932001: New beginnings</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-13"><a href="#1994:_Jacobs_Field_opens"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.1</span> <span class="toctext">1994: Jacobs Field opens</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#1996"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.3</span> <span class="toctext">1996</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#1997:_One_inning_away"><span <span class="toctext">1998\xe2\x80\x932001</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#2002\xe2\x80\x9310:_The_Shapiro/Wedge_years"><span class="tocnumber">2.7</span> <span class="toctext">2002\xe2\x80\x9310: The Shapiro/Wedge years</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-19"><a href="#First_&quot;rebuilding_of_the_team&quot;"><span href="#2011\xe2\x80\x93present:_Antonetti/Francona_era"><span class="tocnumber">2.8</span> <span class="toctext">2011\xe2\x80\x93present: Antonetti/Francona era</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#2016:_Return_to_the_World_Series"><span class="tocnumber">2.8.1</span> <span class="toctext">2016:  Return to the World Series</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23"><a href="#2017:_Record-setting_winning_streak"><span class="tocnumber">2.8.2</span> <span class="toctext">2017: Record-setting winning streak</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Season-by-season_results"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Season-by-season results</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Rivalries"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Rivalries</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Interleague"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Interleague</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Divisional"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Signature songs</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Nickname_and_logo_controversy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Nickname and logo controversy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Cleveland_Indians_in_popular_culture"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Cleveland Indians in popular culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Awards_and_honors"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Awards and honors</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Baseball_Hall_of_Famers"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Baseball Hall of Famers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Ford_C._Frick_Award_recipients"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Ford C. Frick Award recipients</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Retired_numbers"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Retired numbers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Indians_Hall_of_Fame"><span class="tocnumber">10.4</span> <span class="toctext">Indians Hall of Fame</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Statues"><span class="tocnumber">10.5</span> <span class="toctext">Statues</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Franchise_records"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Franchise records</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Season_records"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Season records</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Current_roster"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Current roster</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Minor_league_affiliations"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Minor league affiliations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleveland_baseball_prior_to_the_Indians_franchise">Cleveland baseball prior to the Indians franchise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Cleveland baseball prior to the Indians franchise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>"In 1857 baseball games were a daily spectacle in Cleveland\'s Public Squares. City authorities tried to find an ordinance forbidding it, to the joy of the crowd, they were unsuccessful.&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 Harold Seymour"</i> <sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>1865\xe2\x80\x931868 Forest Citys of Cleveland <i>(Amateur)</i></dt>\n<dt>1869\xe2\x80\x931872 Forest Citys of Cleveland</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forest_Citys.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Forest_Citys.jpg/220px-Forest_Citys.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Forest_Citys.jpg/330px-Forest_Citys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Forest_Citys.jpg/440px-Forest_Citys.jpg 2x" data-file-width="657" data-file-height="502" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forest_Citys.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Forest City Baseball Club</div></div></div>\n<p>From 1865 to 1868 Forest Citys was an amateur ball club. During the <a href="/wiki/1869_in_baseball" title="1869 in baseball">1869</a> season, Cleveland was among several cities which established professional baseball teams following the success of the 1869 <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Red_Stockings" title="Cincinnati Red Stockings">Cincinnati Red Stockings</a>, the first fully professional team.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In the newspapers before and after 1870, the team was often called the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Forest_Citys" title="Cleveland Forest Citys">Forest Citys</a>, in the same generic way that the team from Chicago was sometimes called The Chicagos.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/1871_in_baseball" title="1871 in baseball">1871</a> the Forest Citys joined the new <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Professional_Base_Ball_Players" title="National Association of Professional Base Ball Players">National Association of Professional Base Ball Players</a> (NA), the first professional league. Ultimately, two of the league\'s western clubs went out of business during the first season and the <a href="/wiki/1871_Great_Chicago_Fire" class="mw-redirect" title="1871 Great Chicago Fire">Chicago Fire</a> left that city\'s <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">White Stockings</a> impoverished, unable to field a team again until <a href="/wiki/1874_in_baseball" title="1874 in baseball">1874</a>. Cleveland was thus the NA\'s westernmost outpost in <a href="/wiki/1872_in_baseball" title="1872 in baseball">1872</a>, the year the club folded. Cleveland played their full schedule to July 19 followed by two games versus Boston in mid-August and disbanded at the end of the season.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>1879\xe2\x80\x931881 Cleveland Forest Citys</dt>\n<dt>1882\xe2\x80\x931884 Cleveland Blues</dt></dl>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/1876_in_baseball" title="1876 in baseball">1876</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) supplanted the NA as the major professional league. Cleveland were not among its charter members, but by <a href="/wiki/1879_in_baseball" title="1879 in baseball">1879</a> the league was looking for new entries and the city gained an NL team. The Cleveland Forest Citys baseball team was then re-created. The National League required distinct colors for the 1882 season, so the Cleveland Forest Citys became the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Blues_(NL)" title="Cleveland Blues (NL)">Cleveland Blues</a>. They then had a mediocre record for six seasons and were ruined by a trade war with the <a href="/wiki/Union_Association" title="Union Association">Union Association</a> (UA) in <a href="/wiki/1884_in_baseball" title="1884 in baseball">1884</a>, when its three best players (<a href="/wiki/Fred_Dunlap" title="Fred Dunlap">Fred Dunlap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Glasscock" title="Jack Glasscock">Jack Glasscock</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jim_McCormick_(pitcher)" title="Jim McCormick (pitcher)">Jim McCormick</a>) jumped to the UA after being offered higher salaries. Cleveland Blues merged with the St. Louis Maroons UA team in <a href="/wiki/1885_in_baseball" title="1885 in baseball">1885</a>.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>1887\xe2\x80\x931899 Cleveland Spiders&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 nickname "Blues"</dt></dl>\n<p>Cleveland went without major league baseball for two seasons until gaining a team in the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_(19th_century)" title="American Association (19th century)">American Association</a> (AA) in <a href="/wiki/1887_in_baseball" title="1887 in baseball">1887</a>. After the AA\'s <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Alleghenys" class="mw-redirect" title="Pittsburgh Alleghenys">Allegheny club</a> jumped to the NL Cleveland followed suit in <a href="/wiki/1889_in_baseball" title="1889 in baseball">1889</a>, as the AA began to crumble. The Cleveland ball club, named the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Spiders" title="Cleveland Spiders">Spiders</a> (supposedly inspired by their "skinny and spindly" players) slowly became a power in the league.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The next year the Spiders moved into <a href="/wiki/League_Park" title="League Park">League Park</a>, which would serve as the home of Cleveland professional baseball for the next 55 years. Led by native Ohioan <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young" title="Cy Young">Cy Young</a>, the Spiders became a contender in the mid-1890s, when they played in the <a href="/wiki/Temple_Cup" title="Temple Cup">Temple Cup</a> Series (that era\'s World Series) twice, winning it in <a href="/wiki/1895_in_baseball" title="1895 in baseball">1895</a>. The team began to fade after this success, and was dealt a severe blow under the ownership of the <a href="/wiki/Robison_Field" title="Robison Field">Robison brothers</a>\n</p><p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/1899_in_baseball" title="1899 in baseball">1899</a> season, Frank Robison, the Spiders owner, bought the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Browns_(NL)" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis Browns (NL)">St. Louis Browns</a>, thus owning two clubs at the same time. The Browns were renamed the "Perfectos", and restocked with Cleveland talent. Just weeks before the season opener, most of the better Spiders players were transferred to St. Louis, including three future Hall of Famers: Cy Young, <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Burkett" title="Jesse Burkett">Jesse Burkett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Wallace_(baseball)" title="Bobby Wallace (baseball)">Bobby Wallace</a>.\n<sup id="cite_ref-perfectos_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perfectos-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\nThe roster maneuvers failed to create a powerhouse Perfectos team, as St. Louis finished fifth in both 1899 and <a href="/wiki/1900_in_baseball" title="1900 in baseball">1900</a>. The Spiders were left with essentially a minor league lineup, and began to lose games at a record pace. Drawing almost no fans at home, they ended up playing most of their season on the road, and became known as "The Wanderers."<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The team ended the season in 12th place, 84 games out of first place, with an all-time worst record of 20-134 (.130 winning percentage).<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  Following the 1899 season, the National League disbanded four teams, including the Cleveland franchise. The disastrous 1899 season would actually be a step toward a new future for Cleveland fans the next year.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>1890, Cleveland Infants&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 nickname "Babes"</dt></dl>\n<p>The Cleveland Infants were in the <a href="/wiki/Players%27_League" title="Players&#39; League">Players\' League</a>. The League was well-attended, at least in some cities, but was underfunded and its owners lacked the confidence to continue beyond the one season. There were eight teams who were star-studded; the Boston franchise won the championship. The Cleveland Infants finished with 55 wins and 75 losses.  Their home games were played at <a href="/wiki/Brotherhood_Park" title="Brotherhood Park">Brotherhood Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Franchise_history">Franchise history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Franchise history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Cleveland_Indians" title="History of the Cleveland Indians">History of the Cleveland Indians</a></div>\n<h3><span id="1894.E2.80.931935:_Beginning_to_middle"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1894\xe2\x80\x931935:_Beginning_to_middle">1894\xe2\x80\x931935: Beginning to middle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1894\xe2\x80\x931935: Beginning to middle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <b>Grand Rapids Rustlers</b> were founded in Michigan in 1894 and were part of the <a href="/wiki/Western_League_(1885%E2%80%931899)" title="Western League (1885\xe2\x80\x931899)">Western League</a>.  In 1900 the team moved to Cleveland and was named the <b>Cleveland Lake Shores</b>. Around the same time <a href="/wiki/Ban_Johnson" title="Ban Johnson">Ban Johnson</a> changed the name of his minor league (Western League) to the American League.  In 1900 the American League was still considered a minor league.  In 1901 team was renamed the <b>Cleveland Bluebirds</b> when the American League broke with the National Agreement and declared itself a competing Major League.  The Cleveland franchise was among its eight charter members, and is one of four teams that remain in its original city, along with <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg/170px-Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg/255px-Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg/340px-Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4406" data-file-height="6190" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nap_Lajoie_1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nap_Lajoie" title="Nap Lajoie">Nap Lajoie</a>, who won the 1903 American League Batting Championship with the Indians, was the team\'s namesake from 1903\xe2\x80\x9315, and an <a href="/wiki/MLB_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Hall of Fame">MLB Hall of Famer</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The new team was owned by coal magnate <a href="/wiki/Charles_Somers" title="Charles Somers">Charles Somers</a> and tailor Jack Kilfoyl. Somers, a wealthy industrialist and also co-owner of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Americans" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston Americans">Boston Americans</a>, lent money to other team owners, including <a href="/wiki/Connie_Mack" title="Connie Mack">Connie Mack</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Athletics">Philadelphia Athletics</a>, to keep them and the new league afloat. Players didn\'t think the name "Bluebirds" was suitable for a baseball team.<sup id="cite_ref-TBT_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBT-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Writers frequently shortened it to <b>Cleveland Blues</b> due to the players\' all-blue uniforms,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  but the players didn\'t like this unofficial name either.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  The players themselves tried to change the name to <b>Cleveland Broncos</b> in <a href="/wiki/1902_in_baseball" title="1902 in baseball">1902</a>, but this unofficial name never really caught on.<sup id="cite_ref-TBT_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBT-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Bluebirds suffered from financial problems in their first two seasons. This led Somers to seriously consider moving to either <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a>. Relief came in 1902 as a result of the conflict between the National and American Leagues.  In 1901, <a href="/wiki/Nap_Lajoie" title="Nap Lajoie">Napoleon "Nap" Lajoie</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Phillies" title="Philadelphia Phillies">Philadelphia Phillies</a>\' star second baseman, jumped to the A\'s after his contract was capped at $2,400 per year\xe2\x80\x94one of the highest-profile players to jump to the upstart AL. The Phillies subsequently filed an injunction to force Lajoie\'s return, which was granted by the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Pennsylvania Supreme Court">Pennsylvania Supreme Court</a>. The injunction appeared to doom any hopes of an early settlement between the warring leagues.  However, a lawyer discovered that the injunction was only enforceable in the state of Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-TBT_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBT-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Mack, partly to thank Somers for his past financial support, agreed to trade Lajoie to the then-moribund Blues, who offered $25,000 salary over three years.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>  Due to the injunction, however, Lajoie had to sit out any games played against the A\'s in Philadelphia.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>  Lajoie arrived in Cleveland on June 4 and was an immediate hit, drawing 10,000 fans to League Park.  Soon afterward, he was named team captain, and in 1903 the team was renamed the <b>Cleveland Napoleons</b> (soon shortened to <b>Naps</b>) after a newspaper conducted a write-in contest.<sup id="cite_ref-TBT_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBT-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lajoie was named manager in <a href="/wiki/1905_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1905 Major League Baseball season">1905</a>, and the team\'s fortunes improved somewhat. They finished half a game short of the pennant in 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-1908AL_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1908AL-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>  However, the success did not last and Lajoie resigned during the 1909 season as manager but remained on as a player.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg/220px-1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg/330px-1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg/440px-1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5332" data-file-height="3193" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1909_Cleveland_Naps.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/1909_Cleveland_Naps_season" title="1909 Cleveland Naps season">1909 Cleveland Naps</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After that, the team began to unravel, leading Kilfoyl to sell his share of the team to Somers. <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young" title="Cy Young">Cy Young</a>, who returned to Cleveland in 1909, was ineffective for most of his three remaining years<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Addie_Joss" title="Addie Joss">Addie Joss</a> died from tubercular meningitis prior to the <a href="/wiki/1911_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1911 Major League Baseball season">1911</a> season.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite a strong lineup anchored by the potent Lajoie and <a href="/wiki/Shoeless_Joe_Jackson" title="Shoeless Joe Jackson">Shoeless Joe Jackson</a>, poor pitching kept the team below third place for most of the next decade. One reporter referred to the team as the Napkins, "because they fold up so easily". The team hit bottom in 1914 and 1915, finishing in the cellar both years.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>1915 brought significant changes to the team. Lajoie, nearly 40 years old, was no longer a top hitter in the league, batting only .258 in 1914. With Lajoie engaged in a feud with manager <a href="/wiki/Joe_Birmingham" title="Joe Birmingham">Joe Birmingham</a>, the team sold Lajoie back to the A\'s.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With Lajoie gone, the club needed a new name. Somers asked the local baseball writers to come up with a new name, and based on their input, the team was renamed the Cleveland Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The name referenced the nickname "Indians" that was applied to the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Spiders" title="Cleveland Spiders">Cleveland Spiders</a> baseball club during the time when <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sockalexis" title="Louis Sockalexis">Louis Sockalexis</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a>, played in Cleveland (1897\xe2\x80\x9399).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the same time, Somers\' business ventures began to fail, leaving him deeply in debt. With the Indians playing poorly, attendance and revenue suffered.<sup id="cite_ref-Somers_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Somers-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>  Somers decided to trade Jackson midway through the 1915 season for two players and $31,500, one of the largest sums paid for a player at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By <a href="/wiki/1916_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1916 Major League Baseball season">1916</a>, Somers was at the end of his tether, and sold the team to a syndicate headed by Chicago railroad contractor <a href="/wiki/Jim_Dunn_(baseball_owner)" title="Jim Dunn (baseball owner)">James C. "Jack" Dunn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Somers_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Somers-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>  Manager Lee Fohl, who had taken over in early 1915, acquired two minor league pitchers, <a href="/wiki/Stan_Coveleski" title="Stan Coveleski">Stan Coveleski</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Bagby,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Jim Bagby, Sr.">Jim Bagby</a> and traded for center fielder <a href="/wiki/Tris_Speaker" title="Tris Speaker">Tris Speaker</a>, who was engaged in a salary dispute with the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Red Sox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>  All three would ultimately become key players in bringing a championship to Cleveland.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:right; max-width:808px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg" class="image" title="The 1920 Indians, who won the first World Series Championship in team history."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg/800px-1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg" width="800" height="121" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg/1200px-1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg/1600px-1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4522" data-file-height="685" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1920_Cleveland_Indians_Pano.jpg" title="File:1920 Cleveland Indians Pano.jpg"> </a></div>The 1920 Indians, who won the first World Series Championship in team history.</div>\n</div></div>\n<p>Speaker took over the reins as <a href="/wiki/Player-manager" class="mw-redirect" title="Player-manager">player-manager</a> in <a href="/wiki/1919_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1919 Major League Baseball season">1919</a>, and would lead the team to a championship in 1920. On August 16, the Indians were playing the <a href="/wiki/1920_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1920 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Polo_Grounds" title="Polo Grounds">Polo Grounds</a> in New York. Shortstop <a href="/wiki/Ray_Chapman" title="Ray Chapman">Ray Chapman</a>, who often crowded the plate, was batting against <a href="/wiki/Carl_Mays" title="Carl Mays">Carl Mays</a>, who had an unusual underhand delivery. It was also late in the afternoon and the infield would have been in shadow with the center field area (the batters\' background) bathed in sunlight. As well, at the time, "part of every pitcher\'s job was to dirty up a new ball the moment it was thrown onto the field. By turns, they smeared it with dirt, licorice, tobacco juice; it was deliberately scuffed, sandpapered, scarred, cut, even spiked. The result was a misshapen, earth-colored ball that traveled through the air erratically, tended to soften in the later innings, and as it came over the plate, was very hard to see."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In any case, Chapman did not move reflexively when Mays\' pitch came his way. The pitch hit Chapman in the head, fracturing his skull. Chapman died the next day, becoming the only player to sustain a fatal injury from a pitched ball.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>  The Indians, who at the time were locked in a tight three-way pennant race with the Yankees and <a href="/wiki/1920_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1920 Chicago White Sox season">White Sox</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> were not slowed down by the death of their teammate. Rookie <a href="/wiki/Joe_Sewell" title="Joe Sewell">Joe Sewell</a> hit .329 after replacing Chapman in the lineup.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 1920, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sox_Scandal" title="Black Sox Scandal">Black Sox Scandal</a> came to a boil. With just a few games left in the season, and Cleveland and Chicago neck-and-neck for first place at 94\xe2\x80\x9354 and 95\xe2\x80\x9356 respectively,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> the Chicago owner suspended eight players. The White Sox lost two of three in their final series, while Cleveland won four and lost two in their final two series. Cleveland finished two games ahead of Chicago and three games ahead of the Yankees to win its first pennant, led by Speaker\'s .388 hitting, Jim Bagby\'s 30 victories and solid performances from <a href="/wiki/Steve_O%27Neill" title="Steve O&#39;Neill">Steve O\'Neill</a> and Stan Coveleski. Cleveland went on to defeat the <a href="/wiki/1920_Brooklyn_Robins_season" title="1920 Brooklyn Robins season">Brooklyn Robins</a> 5\xe2\x80\x932 in the <a href="/wiki/1920_World_Series" title="1920 World Series">World Series</a> for their first title, winning four games in a row after the Robins took a 2\xe2\x80\x931 Series lead. The Series included three memorable "firsts", all of them in Game 5 at Cleveland, and all by the home team. In the first inning, right fielder <a href="/wiki/Elmer_Smith_(20th_century_outfielder)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elmer Smith (20th century outfielder)">Elmer Smith</a> hit the first Series grand slam. In the fourth inning, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Bagby,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Jim Bagby, Sr.">Jim Bagby</a> hit the first Series home run by a pitcher. And in the top of the fifth inning, second baseman <a href="/wiki/Bill_Wambsganss" title="Bill Wambsganss">Bill Wambsganss</a> executed the first (and only, so far) unassisted triple play in World Series history, in fact the only Series triple play of any kind.\n</p><p>The team would not reach the heights of 1920 again for 28 years. Speaker and Coveleski were aging and the Yankees were rising with a new weapon: <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> and the home run. They managed two second-place finishes but spent much of the decade in the cellar. In 1927 Dunn\'s widow, Mrs. George Pross (Dunn had died in 1922), sold the team to a syndicate headed by <a href="/wiki/Alva_Bradley" title="Alva Bradley">Alva Bradley</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1936.E2.80.9346:_Bob_Feller_enters_the_show"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1936\xe2\x80\x9346:_Bob_Feller_enters_the_show">1936\xe2\x80\x9346: Bob Feller enters the show</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1936\xe2\x80\x9346: Bob Feller enters the show">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Indians were a middling team by the 1930s, finishing third or fourth most years. <a href="/wiki/1936_in_baseball" title="1936 in baseball">1936</a> brought Cleveland a new superstar in 17-year-old pitcher <a href="/wiki/Bob_Feller" title="Bob Feller">Bob Feller</a>, who came from <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a> with a dominating <a href="/wiki/Fastball" title="Fastball">fastball</a>. That season, Feller set a record with 17 strikeouts in a single game and went on to lead the league in strikeouts from 1938\xe2\x80\x931941.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bob_Feller.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bob_Feller.jpg/220px-Bob_Feller.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bob_Feller.jpg/330px-Bob_Feller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bob_Feller.jpg/440px-Bob_Feller.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bob_Feller.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bob_Feller" title="Bob Feller">Bob Feller</a>; winner of the A.L. pitching <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Triple_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Triple Crown">Triple Crown</a> in 1940, member of the <a href="/wiki/1948_World_Series" title="1948 World Series">1948 World Series</a> Championship team, the Indians all-time leader in wins and strikeouts, and an <a href="/wiki/MLB_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Hall of Fame">MLB Hall of Famer</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>On August 20, 1938, Indians catchers <a href="/wiki/Hank_Helf" title="Hank Helf">Hank Helf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Pytlak" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Pytlak">Frank Pytlak</a> set the "all-time altitude mark" by catching baseballs dropped from the 708-foot <a href="/wiki/Terminal_Tower" title="Terminal Tower">Terminal Tower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By <a href="/wiki/1940_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1940 Major League Baseball season">1940</a>, Feller, along with <a href="/wiki/Ken_Keltner" title="Ken Keltner">Ken Keltner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mel_Harder" title="Mel Harder">Mel Harder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lou_Boudreau" title="Lou Boudreau">Lou Boudreau</a> led the Indians to within one game of the pennant. However, the team was wracked with dissension, with some players (including Feller and Mel Harder) going so far as to request that Bradley fire manager <a href="/wiki/Ossie_Vitt" title="Ossie Vitt">Ossie Vitt</a>. Reporters lampooned them as the Cleveland Crybabies.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (August 2012)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Feller, who had pitched a <a href="/wiki/No-hitter" title="No-hitter">no-hitter</a> to open the season and won 27 games, lost the final game of the season to unknown pitcher Floyd Giebell of the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a>. The <a href="/wiki/1940_Detroit_Tigers_season" title="1940 Detroit Tigers season">Tigers</a> won the pennant and Giebell never won another major league game.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cleveland entered <a href="/wiki/1941_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1941 Major League Baseball season">1941</a> with a young team and a new manager; <a href="/wiki/Roger_Peckinpaugh" title="Roger Peckinpaugh">Roger Peckinpaugh</a> had replaced the despised Vitt; but the team regressed, finishing in fourth. Cleveland would soon be depleted of two stars. <a href="/wiki/Hal_Trosky" title="Hal Trosky">Hal Trosky</a> retired in 1941 due to migraine headaches<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and Bob Feller enlisted in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a> two days after the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">Attack on Pearl Harbor</a>.  Starting third baseman <a href="/wiki/Ken_Keltner" title="Ken Keltner">Ken Keltner</a> and outfielder <a href="/wiki/Ray_Mack" title="Ray Mack">Ray Mack</a> were both drafted in 1945 taking two more starters out of the lineup.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1946.E2.80.9349:_The_Bill_Veeck_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1946\xe2\x80\x9349:_The_Bill_Veeck_years">1946\xe2\x80\x9349: The Bill Veeck years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1946\xe2\x80\x9349: The Bill Veeck years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/1946_in_baseball" title="1946 in baseball">1946</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Veeck" title="Bill Veeck">Bill Veeck</a> formed an investment group that purchased the Cleveland Indians from Bradley\'s group for a reported $1.6 million.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Among the investors was <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hope" title="Bob Hope">Bob Hope</a>, who had grown up in Cleveland, and former Tigers slugger, <a href="/wiki/Hank_Greenberg" title="Hank Greenberg">Hank Greenberg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\nA former owner of a minor league franchise in Milwaukee, Veeck brought to Cleveland a gift for promotion. At one point, Veeck hired rubber-faced<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Max_Patkin" title="Max Patkin">Max Patkin</a>, the "Clown Prince of Baseball" as a coach. Patkin\'s appearance in the coaching box was the sort of promotional stunt that delighted fans but infuriated the American League front office.\n</p><p>Recognizing that he had acquired a solid team, Veeck soon abandoned the aging, small and lightless League Park to take up full-time residence in massive <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Municipal_Stadium" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleveland Municipal Stadium">Cleveland Municipal Stadium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borsvold_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borsvold-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The Indians had briefly moved from League Park to Municipal Stadium in mid-1932, but moved back to League Park due to complaints about the cavernous environment.  From 1937 onward, however, the Indians began playing an increasing number of games at Municipal, until by 1940 they played most of their home slate there.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> League Park was mostly demolished in 1951, but has since been rebuilt as a recreational park.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Logo_of_the_Cleveland_Indians_(1946-1950).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Logo_of_the_Cleveland_Indians_%281946-1950%29.png/220px-Logo_of_the_Cleveland_Indians_%281946-1950%29.png" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Logo_of_the_Cleveland_Indians_%281946-1950%29.png/330px-Logo_of_the_Cleveland_Indians_%281946-1950%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Logo_of_the_Cleveland_Indians_%281946-1950%29.png 2x" data-file-width="368" data-file-height="483" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Logo_of_the_Cleveland_Indians_(1946-1950).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cleveland Indians logo from 1946\xe2\x80\x9350</div></div></div>\n<p>Making the most of the cavernous stadium, Veeck had a portable center field fence installed, which he could move in or out depending on how the distance favored the Indians against their opponents in a given series. The fence moved as much as 15 feet (5&#160;m) between series opponents. Following the 1947 season, the American League countered with a rule change that fixed the distance of an outfield wall for the duration of a season.  The massive stadium did, however, permit the Indians to set the then record for the largest crowd to see a Major League baseball game.  On October 10, 1948, Game 5 of the <a href="/wiki/1948_World_Series" title="1948 World Series">World Series</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Braves_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston Braves (baseball)">Boston Braves</a> drew over 84,000. The record stood until the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> drew a crowd in excess of 92,500 to watch Game 5 of the <a href="/wiki/1959_World_Series" title="1959 World Series">1959 World Series</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Memorial_Coliseum" title="Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum">Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a>.\n</p><p>Under Veeck\'s leadership, one of Cleveland\'s most significant achievements was breaking the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_color_line" title="Baseball color line">color barrier</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> by signing <a href="/wiki/Larry_Doby" title="Larry Doby">Larry Doby</a>, formerly a player for the Negro League\'s <a href="/wiki/Newark_Eagles" title="Newark Eagles">Newark Eagles</a> in <a href="/wiki/1947_in_baseball" title="1947 in baseball">1947</a>, 11 weeks after <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a> signed with the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Dodgers" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooklyn Dodgers">Dodgers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borsvold_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borsvold-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  Similar to Robinson, Doby battled racism on and off the field but posted a .301 batting average in 1948, his first full season. A power-hitting center fielder, Doby led the American League twice in homers.\n</p><p>In 1948, needing pitching for the stretch run of the pennant race, Veeck turned to the <a href="/wiki/Negro_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Negro League">Negro League</a> again and signed pitching great <a href="/wiki/Satchel_Paige" title="Satchel Paige">Satchel Paige</a> amid much controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-Borsvold_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borsvold-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  Barred from Major League Baseball during his prime, Veeck\'s signing of the aging star in 1948 was viewed by many as another publicity stunt. At an official age of 42, Paige became the oldest rookie in Major League baseball history, and the first black pitcher. Paige ended the year with a 6\xe2\x80\x931 record with a 2.48 ERA, 45 strikeouts and two shutouts.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LouBoudreau1953bowman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/LouBoudreau1953bowman.jpg/180px-LouBoudreau1953bowman.jpg" width="180" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/LouBoudreau1953bowman.jpg/270px-LouBoudreau1953bowman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/LouBoudreau1953bowman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="275" data-file-height="415" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LouBoudreau1953bowman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lou_Boudreau" title="Lou Boudreau">Lou Boudreau</a>, 1948 American League MVP</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/1948_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1948 Major League Baseball season">1948</a>, veterans Boudreau, Keltner, and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Gordon" title="Joe Gordon">Joe Gordon</a> had career offensive seasons, while newcomers Doby and <a href="/wiki/Gene_Bearden" title="Gene Bearden">Gene Bearden</a> also had standout seasons. The team went down to the wire with the <a href="/wiki/1948_Boston_Red_Sox_season" title="1948 Boston Red Sox season">Boston Red Sox</a>, winning a one-game playoff, the first in American League history, to go to the <a href="/wiki/1948_World_Series" title="1948 World Series">World Series</a>. In the series, the Indians defeated the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Braves#History" title="Atlanta Braves">Boston Braves</a> four games to two for their first championship in 28 years. Boudreau won the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">American League MVP Award</a>.\n</p><p>The Indians would appear in a film the following <a href="/wiki/1949_in_film" title="1949 in film">year</a> titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kid_From_Cleveland" class="mw-redirect" title="The Kid From Cleveland">The Kid From Cleveland</a></i>, in which Veeck had an interest.<sup id="cite_ref-Borsvold_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borsvold-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  The film portrayed the team helping out a "troubled teenaged fan"<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and featured many members of the Indians organization. However, filming during the season cost the players valuable rest days leading to fatigue towards the end of the season.<sup id="cite_ref-Borsvold_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borsvold-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  That season, Cleveland again contended before falling to third place. On September 23, 1949, Bill Veeck and the Indians buried their 1948 pennant in center field the day after they were mathematically eliminated from the pennant race.<sup id="cite_ref-Borsvold_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borsvold-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later in 1949, Veeck\'s first wife (who had a half-stake in Veeck\'s share of the team) divorced him. With most of his money tied up in the Indians, Veeck was forced to sell the team<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> to a syndicate headed by insurance magnate Ellis Ryan.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1950.E2.80.9359:_Near_misses"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1950\xe2\x80\x9359:_Near_misses">1950\xe2\x80\x9359: Near misses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1950\xe2\x80\x9359: Near misses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Rosen_1953.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Al_Rosen_1953.jpg/180px-Al_Rosen_1953.jpg" width="180" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Al_Rosen_1953.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="252" data-file-height="391" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_Rosen_1953.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Al_Rosen" title="Al Rosen">Al Rosen</a>, 1953 Most Valuable Player.</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/1953_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1953 Major League Baseball season">1953</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Rosen" title="Al Rosen">Al Rosen</a> was an All Star for the second year in a row, was named <i>The Sporting News</i> Major League Player of the Year, and won the <a href="/wiki/American_League_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="American League Most Valuable Player Award">American League Most Valuable Player Award</a> in a unanimous vote playing for the Indians after leading the AL in runs, home runs, RBIs (for the second year in a row), and slugging percentage, and coming in second by one point in batting average.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Ryan was forced out in 1953 in favor of Myron Wilson, who in turn gave way to William Daley in <a href="/wiki/1956_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1956 Major League Baseball season">1956</a>.  Despite this turnover in the ownership, a powerhouse team composed of Feller, Doby, <a href="/wiki/Minnie_Mi%C3%B1oso" title="Minnie Mi\xc3\xb1oso">Minnie Mi\xc3\xb1oso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luke_Easter_(baseball)" title="Luke Easter (baseball)">Luke Easter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_%C3%81vila" title="Bobby \xc3\x81vila">Bobby \xc3\x81vila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Rosen" title="Al Rosen">Al Rosen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Early_Wynn" title="Early Wynn">Early Wynn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Lemon" title="Bob Lemon">Bob Lemon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mike_Garcia_(AL_pitcher)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mike Garcia (AL pitcher)">Mike Garcia</a> continued to contend through the early 1950s. However, Cleveland only won a single pennant in the decade, in <a href="/wiki/1954_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1954 Major League Baseball season">1954</a>, finishing second to the <a href="/wiki/1954_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1954 New York Yankees season">New York Yankees</a> five times.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herb_Score_1955.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Herb_Score_1955.JPG/170px-Herb_Score_1955.JPG" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Herb_Score_1955.JPG/255px-Herb_Score_1955.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Herb_Score_1955.JPG/340px-Herb_Score_1955.JPG 2x" data-file-width="893" data-file-height="1325" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herb_Score_1955.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Herb_Score" title="Herb Score">Herb Score</a> \xe2\x80\x93 who was the 1955 <a href="/wiki/American_League_Rookie_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="American League Rookie of the Year">American League Rookie of the Year</a>, a two-time A.L. All-Star, and after his playing career went on to be the longest tenured announcer in club history, serving 34 seasons (1964\xe2\x80\x931997) as a member of the Indians broadcast team.</div></div></div>\n<p>The winningest season in franchise history came in 1954, when the <a href="/wiki/1954_Cleveland_Indians_season" title="1954 Cleveland Indians season">Indians</a> finished the season with a record of 111\xe2\x80\x9343 (.721). That mark set an American League record for wins which stood for 44 years until the <a href="/wiki/1998_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1998 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> won 114 games in <a href="/wiki/1998_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1998 Major League Baseball season">1998</a> (a 162-game regular season). The Indians 1954 winning percentage of .721 is still an American League record. The Indians returned to the <a href="/wiki/1954_World_Series" title="1954 World Series">World Series</a> to face the <a href="/wiki/1954_New_York_Giants_(MLB)_season" title="1954 New York Giants (MLB) season">New York Giants</a>. The team could not bring home the title, however, ultimately being upset by the Giants in a sweep. The series was notable for <a href="/wiki/Willie_Mays" title="Willie Mays">Willie Mays</a>\' <a href="/wiki/The_Catch_(baseball)" title="The Catch (baseball)">over-the-shoulder catch</a> off the bat of <a href="/wiki/Vic_Wertz" title="Vic Wertz">Vic Wertz</a> in Game 1. Cleveland remained a talented team throughout the remainder of the decade, finishing in second place in 1959, <a href="/wiki/George_Strickland_(baseball)" title="George Strickland (baseball)">George Strickland</a>\'s last full year in the majors.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1960.E2.80.9393:_The_33-year_slump"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1960\xe2\x80\x9393:_The_33-year_slump">1960\xe2\x80\x9393: The 33-year slump</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1960\xe2\x80\x9393: The 33-year slump">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From 1960 to 1993, the Indians managed one third-place finish (in 1968) and six fourth-place finishes (in 1960, 1974 to 1976, 1990, and 1992) but spent the rest of the time at or near the bottom of the standings.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Frank_Lane_becomes_general_manager">Frank Lane becomes general manager</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Frank Lane becomes general manager">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Indians hired general manager <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lane" title="Frank Lane">Frank Lane</a>, known as "Trader" Lane, away from the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a> in <a href="/wiki/1957_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1957 Major League Baseball season">1957</a>. Lane over the years had gained a reputation as a GM who loved to make deals.  With the White Sox, Lane had made over 100 trades involving over 400 players in seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-Cards_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cards-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>  In a short stint in St. Louis, he traded away <a href="/wiki/Red_Schoendienst" title="Red Schoendienst">Red Schoendienst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Haddix" title="Harvey Haddix">Harvey Haddix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cards_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cards-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>  Lane summed up his philosophy when he said that the only deals he regretted were the ones that he didn\'t make.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of Lane\'s early trades in Cleveland was to send <a href="/wiki/Roger_Maris" title="Roger Maris">Roger Maris</a> to the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Kansas_City_Athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Kansas City Athletics">Kansas City Athletics</a> in the middle of <a href="/wiki/1958_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1958 Major League Baseball season">1958</a>. Indians executive <a href="/wiki/Hank_Greenberg" title="Hank Greenberg">Hank Greenberg</a> was not happy about the trade<sup id="cite_ref-Maris_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maris-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and neither was Maris, who said that he could not stand Lane.<sup id="cite_ref-Maris_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maris-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>  After Maris broke Babe Ruth\'s home run record, Lane defended himself by saying he still would have done the deal because Maris was unknown and he received good ballplayers in exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-Maris_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maris-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the Maris trade, Lane acquired 25-year-old <a href="/wiki/Norm_Cash" title="Norm Cash">Norm Cash</a> from the White Sox for <a href="/wiki/Minnie_Mi%C3%B1oso" title="Minnie Mi\xc3\xb1oso">Minnie Mi\xc3\xb1oso</a> and then traded him to Detroit before he ever played a game for the Indians; Cash went on to hit over 350 home runs for the Tigers. The Indians received <a href="/wiki/Steve_Demeter" title="Steve Demeter">Steve Demeter</a> in the deal, who would have only five at-bats for Cleveland.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Curse_of_Rocky_Colavito">Curse of Rocky Colavito</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Curse of Rocky Colavito">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Curse_of_Rocky_Colavito" title="Curse of Rocky Colavito">Curse of Rocky Colavito</a></div>\n<p>In 1960, Lane made the trade that would define his tenure in Cleveland when he dealt slugging right fielder and fan favorite<sup id="cite_ref-Colavito_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colavito-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Colavito" title="Rocky Colavito">Rocky Colavito</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a> for <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Kuenn" title="Harvey Kuenn">Harvey Kuenn</a> just before Opening Day in <a href="/wiki/1960_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1960 Major League Baseball season">1960</a>.\n</p><p>It was a blockbuster trade that swapped the <a href="/wiki/1959_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1959 Major League Baseball season">1959</a> AL home run co-champion (Colavito) for the AL batting champion (Kuenn). After the trade, however, Colavito hit over 30 home runs four times and made three All-Star teams for Detroit and Kansas City before returning to Cleveland in <a href="/wiki/1965_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1965 Major League Baseball season">1965</a>. Kuenn, on the other hand, would play only one season for the Indians before departing for <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Giants" title="San Francisco Giants">San Francisco</a> in a trade for an aging <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Antonelli" title="Johnny Antonelli">Johnny Antonelli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Kirkland" title="Willie Kirkland">Willie Kirkland</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Akron_Beacon_Journal" title="Akron Beacon Journal">Akron Beacon Journal</a></i> columnist <a href="/wiki/Terry_Pluto" title="Terry Pluto">Terry Pluto</a> documented the decades of woe that followed the trade in his book <i>The Curse of Rocky Colavito</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>  Despite being attached to the curse, Colavito said that he never placed a curse on the Indians but that the trade was prompted by a salary dispute with Lane.<sup id="cite_ref-Curse_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curse-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lane also engineered a unique trade of managers in mid-season 1960, sending <a href="/wiki/Joe_Gordon" title="Joe Gordon">Joe Gordon</a> to the Tigers in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Dykes" title="Jimmy Dykes">Jimmy Dykes</a>. Lane left the team in 1961, but ill-advised trades continued. In 1965, the Indians traded pitcher <a href="/wiki/Tommy_John" title="Tommy John">Tommy John</a>, who would go on to win 288 games in his career, and 1966 Rookie of the Year <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Agee" class="mw-redirect" title="Tommy Agee">Tommy Agee</a> to the White Sox to get Colavito back.<sup id="cite_ref-Curse_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curse-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Indians\' pitchers also set numerous strikeout records. They led the league in K\'s every year from 1963 to 1968, and narrowly missed in 1969. The 1964 staff was the first to amass 1,100 strikeouts, and in 1968, they were the first to collect more strikeouts than hits allowed.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1969:_Move_to_the_AL_East_division">1969: Move to the AL East division</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1969: Move to the AL East division">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The 1970s were not much better, with the Indians trading away several future stars, including <a href="/wiki/Graig_Nettles" title="Graig Nettles">Graig Nettles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Eckersley" title="Dennis Eckersley">Dennis Eckersley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Bell" title="Buddy Bell">Buddy Bell</a> and 1971 Rookie of the Year <a href="/wiki/Chris_Chambliss" title="Chris Chambliss">Chris Chambliss</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> for a number of players who made no impact.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Constant ownership changes did not help the Indians. In 1963, Daley\'s syndicate sold the team to a group headed by general manager <a href="/wiki/Gabe_Paul" title="Gabe Paul">Gabe Paul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TBT_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBT-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  Three years later, Paul sold the Indians to <a href="/wiki/Vernon_Stouffer" title="Vernon Stouffer">Vernon Stouffer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Stouffer%27s" title="Stouffer&#39;s">Stouffer\'s</a> frozen-food empire. Prior to Stouffer\'s purchase, the team was rumored to be relocated due to poor attendance. Despite the potential for a financially strong owner, Stouffer had some non-baseball related financial setbacks and, consequently, the team was cash-poor.  In order to solve some financial problems, Stouffer had made an agreement to play a minimum of 30 home games in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> with a view to a possible move there.<sup id="cite_ref-Mileti_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mileti-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>  After rejecting an offer from <a href="/wiki/George_Steinbrenner" title="George Steinbrenner">George Steinbrenner</a> and former Indian <a href="/wiki/Al_Rosen" title="Al Rosen">Al Rosen</a>, Stouffer sold the team in 1972 to a group led by <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Cavaliers" title="Cleveland Cavaliers">Cleveland Cavaliers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Barons_(1937%E2%80%931973)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleveland Barons (1937\xe2\x80\x931973)">Cleveland Barons</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Nick_Mileti" title="Nick Mileti">Nick Mileti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mileti_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mileti-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>  Steinbrenner went on to buy the New York Yankees in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Only five years later, Mileti\'s group sold the team for $11 million to a syndicate headed by trucking magnate Steve O\'Neill and including former general manager and owner Gabe Paul.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>  O\'Neill\'s death in 1983 led to the team going on the market once more. O\'Neill\'s nephew Patrick O\'Neill did not find a buyer until real estate magnates <a href="/wiki/Richard_Jacobs_(businessman)" title="Richard Jacobs (businessman)">Richard</a> and David Jacobs purchased the team in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The team was unable to move out of the cellar, with losing seasons between 1969 and 1975. One highlight was the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Gaylord_Perry" title="Gaylord Perry">Gaylord Perry</a> in <a href="/wiki/1972_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1972 Major League Baseball season">1972</a>. The Indians traded fireballer <a href="/wiki/Sam_McDowell" title="Sam McDowell">"Sudden Sam" McDowell</a> for Perry, who became the first Indian pitcher to win the <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Award</a>. In <a href="/wiki/1975_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1975 Major League Baseball season">1975</a>, Cleveland broke another color barrier with the hiring of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Robinson" title="Frank Robinson">Frank Robinson</a> as Major League Baseball\'s first African American manager. Robinson served as player-manager and would provide a franchise highlight when he hit a pinch hit home run on Opening Day. But the high-profile signing of <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Garland" title="Wayne Garland">Wayne Garland</a>, a 20-game winner in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore</a>, proved to be a disaster after Garland suffered from shoulder problems and went 28\xe2\x80\x9348 over five years.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The team failed to improve with Robinson as manager and he was fired in <a href="/wiki/1977_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1977 Major League Baseball season">1977</a>. In 1977, pitcher <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Eckersley" title="Dennis Eckersley">Dennis Eckersley</a> threw a no-hitter against the <a href="/wiki/1977_California_Angels_season" title="1977 California Angels season">California Angels</a>. The next season, he would be dealt to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a> where he won 20 games in 1978 and another 17 in 1979.\n</p><p>The 1970s also featured the infamous <a href="/wiki/Ten_Cent_Beer_Night" title="Ten Cent Beer Night">Ten Cent Beer Night</a> at Cleveland Municipal Stadium. The ill-conceived promotion at a <a href="/wiki/1974_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1974 Major League Baseball season">1974</a> game against the <a href="/wiki/1974_Texas_Rangers_season" title="1974 Texas Rangers season">Texas Rangers</a> ended in a riot by fans and a forfeit by the Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were more bright spots in the 1980s. In May 1981, <a href="/wiki/Len_Barker" title="Len Barker">Len Barker</a> threw a <a href="/wiki/Perfect_game" title="Perfect game">perfect game</a> against the <a href="/wiki/1981_Toronto_Blue_Jays_season" title="1981 Toronto Blue Jays season">Toronto Blue Jays</a>, joining Addie Joss as the only other Indian pitcher to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-Barker_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barker-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Joe_Charboneau" title="Joe Charboneau">"Super Joe" Charbonneau</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Rookie_of_the_Year_Award" title="Major League Baseball Rookie of the Year Award">American League Rookie of the Year</a> award. Unfortunately, Charboneau was out of baseball by 1983 after falling victim to back injuries<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and Barker, who was also hampered by injuries, never became a consistently dominant starting pitcher.<sup id="cite_ref-Barker_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barker-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Eventually, the Indians traded Barker to the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Braves" title="Atlanta Braves">Atlanta Braves</a> for <a href="/wiki/Brett_Butler_(baseball)" title="Brett Butler (baseball)">Brett Butler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brook_Jacoby" title="Brook Jacoby">Brook Jacoby</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Barker_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barker-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> who would become mainstays of the team for the remainder of the decade. Butler and Jacoby were joined by <a href="/wiki/Joe_Carter" title="Joe Carter">Joe Carter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mel_Hall" title="Mel Hall">Mel Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julio_Franco" title="Julio Franco">Julio Franco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cory_Snyder" title="Cory Snyder">Cory Snyder</a>, which brought new hope to fans in the late 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cleveland\'s struggles over the 30-year span were highlighted in the 1989 film <i><a href="/wiki/Major_League_(film)" title="Major League (film)">Major League</a></i>, which comically depicted a hapless Cleveland ball club going from worst to first by the end of the film.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_turnaround">Organizational turnaround</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Organizational turnaround">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Throughout the 1980s, the Indians\' owners had pushed for a new stadium. Cleveland Stadium had been a symbol of the Indians\' glory years in the 1940s and 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> However, during the lean years even crowds of 40,000 were swallowed up by the cavernous environment. The old stadium was not aging gracefully; chunks of concrete were falling off in sections and the old wooden pilings now petrified.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>  In 1984, a proposal for a $150 million domed stadium was defeated in a referendum 2\xe2\x80\x931.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Finally, in May 1990, <a href="/wiki/Cuyahoga_County,_Ohio" title="Cuyahoga County, Ohio">Cuyahoga County</a> voters passed an <a href="/wiki/Pigovian_tax" title="Pigovian tax">excise tax</a> on sales of alcohol and cigarettes in the county. The tax proceeds would be used to finance the building of the <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Sports_and_Entertainment_Complex" title="Gateway Sports and Entertainment Complex">Gateway Sports and Entertainment Complex</a> which would include <a href="/wiki/Jacobs_Field" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacobs Field">Jacobs Field</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quicken_Loans_Arena" title="Quicken Loans Arena">Gund Arena</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Cavaliers" title="Cleveland Cavaliers">Cleveland Cavaliers</a> basketball team.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>  The team had new ownership and a new stadium on the way. They now needed a winning team.\n</p><p>The team\'s fortunes started to turn in <a href="/wiki/1989_in_baseball" title="1989 in baseball">1989</a>, ironically with a very unpopular trade. The team sent power-hitting outfielder <a href="/wiki/Joe_Carter" title="Joe Carter">Joe Carter</a> to the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Padres" title="San Diego Padres">San Diego Padres</a> for two unproven players, <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Alomar,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Sandy Alomar, Jr.">Sandy Alomar, Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Baerga" title="Carlos Baerga">Carlos Baerga</a>. Alomar made an immediate impact, not only being elected to the <a href="/wiki/1990_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="1990 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star team</a> but also winning Cleveland\'s fourth <a href="/wiki/MLB_Rookie_of_the_Year_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Rookie of the Year Award">Rookie of the Year</a> award and a <a href="/wiki/Gold_Glove_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Gold Glove Award">Gold Glove</a>. Baerga would become a three-time All-Star with consistent offensive production.\n</p><p>Indians general manager <a href="/wiki/John_Hart_(baseball)" title="John Hart (baseball)">John Hart</a> made a number of moves that would finally bring success to the team. In <a href="/wiki/1991_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1991 Major League Baseball season">1991</a>, he hired former Indians <a href="/wiki/Mike_Hargrove" title="Mike Hargrove">Mike Hargrove</a> to manage and traded catcher <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Taubensee" title="Eddie Taubensee">Eddie Taubensee</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Houston_Astros" title="Houston Astros">Houston Astros</a> who, with a surplus of outfielders, were willing to part with <a href="/wiki/Kenny_Lofton" title="Kenny Lofton">Kenny Lofton</a>. Lofton finished second in AL Rookie of the Year balloting with a .285 average and 66 <a href="/wiki/Stolen_bases" class="mw-redirect" title="Stolen bases">stolen bases</a>.\n</p><p>The Indians were named "Organization of the Year" by <i>Baseball America</i> <sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> in 1992, in response to the appearance of offensive bright spots and an improving <a href="/wiki/Farm_team" title="Farm team">farm system</a>.\n</p><p>The team suffered a tragedy during spring training of <a href="/wiki/1993_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1993 Major League Baseball season">1993</a>, when a boat carrying pitchers <a href="/wiki/Steve_Olin" title="Steve Olin">Steve Olin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tim_Crews" title="Tim Crews">Tim Crews</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Ojeda" title="Bob Ojeda">Bob Ojeda</a> crashed into a pier. Olin and Crews were killed, and Ojeda was seriously injured. (Ojeda missed most of the season, and would retire the following year).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of the 1993 season, the team was in transition, leaving Cleveland Stadium and fielding a talented nucleus of young players. Many of those players came from the Indians\' new <a href="/wiki/AAA_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="AAA (baseball)">AAA</a> farm team, the <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Knights" title="Charlotte Knights">Charlotte Knights</a>, who won the <a href="/wiki/International_League" title="International League">International League</a> title that year.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1994.E2.80.932001:_New_beginnings"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1994\xe2\x80\x932001:_New_beginnings">1994\xe2\x80\x932001: New beginnings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cleveland_Indians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 1994\xe2\x80\x932001: New beginnings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1994:_Jacobs_Field_opens">1994: Jacobs Field opens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
